import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from einops import rearrange

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head, d_state, block_len=64):
        """
        Mamba Structured State Space model implementation for benchmarking.
        
        :param batch_size: Size of the batch
        :param seq_length: Length of the input sequence
        :param n_heads: Number of attention heads
        :param d_head: Dimension of each head
        :param d_state: Dimension of the state space
        :param block_len: Length of each block for chunked computation
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        assert seq_length % block_len == 0, "Sequence length must be divisible by block length"
        
        self.batch_size = batch_size
        self.seq_length = seq_length
        self.n_heads = n_heads
        self.d_head = d_head
        self.d_state = d_state
        self.block_len = block_len
        
        # Initialize parameters
        self.A = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads))
        self.B = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state))
        self.C = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state))
        
    def segsum(self, x):
        """Naive segment sum calculation."""
        T = x.size(-1)
        x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1)
        x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :]
        mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T, T, device=x.device, dtype=bool), diagonal=0)
        x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -torch.inf)
        return x_segsum
    
    def forward(self, X, initial_states=None):
        """
        Forward pass implementing the SSD operation.
        
        :param X: Input tensor of shape (batch, length, n_heads, d_head)
        :param initial_states: Optional initial states
        :return: Output tensor Y and final state
        """
        # Rearrange into blocks/chunks
        X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [
            rearrange(x, "b (c l) ... -> b c l ...", l=self.block_len)
            for x in (X, self.A, self.B, self.C)
        ]
        
        A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l")
        A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1)
        
        # 1. Compute diagonal block outputs
        L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks))
        Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", 
                             C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks)
        
        # 2. Compute intra-chunk states
        decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum))
        states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", 
                            B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks)
        
        # 3. Compute inter-chunk recurrence
        if initial_states is None:
            initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1])
        states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1)
        
        decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0))))
        new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states)
        return new_states[:, -1]

# Test parameters
batch_size = 2048
seq_length = 128
n_heads = 8
d_head = 64
d_state = 16
block_len = 64

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head, d_state, block_len]

For j=0, i=c: `cumsum[c] - cumsum[0]`. For j=1, i=c: `cumsum[c] - cumsum[1]`. ... So actually: `M[i,j] = sum_{k=j+1}^i x[k]` for j <= i. (with the convention that empty sum is 0) And `M[i,i] = 0` (since cumsum[i] - cumsum[i] = 0). So for padded_chunk_vec = [0, v0, v1, ..., v_{c-1}]: cumsum = [0, v0, v0+v1, ..., sum_{k=0}^{c-1} v_k] M[c, 0] = cumsum[c] - cumsum[0] = sum of all v's M[c, 1] = cumsum[c] - cumsum[1] = sum_{k=1}^{c-1} v_k ... And `states_cat` = [initial_states, states[:,0], states[:,1], ..., states[:,c-1]] So `new_states[:, -1] = sum_{j=0}^{c} exp(M[c, j]) * states_cat[:, j]` = `exp(M[c,0]) * initial_states + sum_{j=1}^{c} exp(M[c,j]) * states[:, j-1]` But initial_states is zero, so the j=0 term vanishes. = `sum_{j=0}^{c-1} exp(M[c, j+1]) * states[:, j]` = `sum_{j=0}^{c-1} exp(cumsum[c] - cumsum[j+1]) * states[:, j]` Since cumsum[j+1] = sum_{k=0}^j v_k = prefix_sum of v up to j. So if we let `chunk_decays = cumsum[-1] - cumsum[1:]` = `cumsum[-1:] - cumsum[1:]` shape `(b, h, c)`. Wait, cumsum is `(b, h, c+1)`, so `cumsum[:,:,-1:]` is `(b, h, 1)`, `cumsum[:,:,1:]` is `(b, h, c)`. Broadcast subtract -> `(b, h, c)`. Then `exp(chunk_decays)` gives `(b, h, c)` and `new_states[:, -1] = sum_{j=0}^{c-1} exp(chunk_decays[:,:,j]) * states[:, j]`. So we don't need the full segsum and matrix multiply for chunks! We just need: `chunk_vec = A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1]` -> `(b, h, c)` `padded = F.pad(chunk_vec, (1, 0))` -> `(b, h, c+1)` `chunk_cumsum = torch.cumsum(padded, dim=-1)` -> `(b, h, c+1)` `chunk_decays = chunk_cumsum[:, :, -1:] - chunk_cumsum[:, :, 1:]` -> `(b, h, c)` `decay_chunk = torch.exp(chunk_decays)` -> `(b, h, c)` Then `final_state = einsum("bhc,bchpn->bhpn", decay_chunk, states)` -> `(b, h, d_head, d_state)` This is a huge simplification! Instead of computing a `(c+1, c+1)` segsum and a `(c+1, c+1)` x `(b, c+1, h, p, n)` einsum, we just compute a vector of size c per batch/head and do a simple contraction. But wait, does this match numerically? Yes, mathematically it's identical. There's also the diagonal block outputs step which we can skip entirely. So the optimized computation is: 1. Reshape X, A, B, C into blocks 2. Compute A_cumsum = cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1) 3. Compute decay_states = exp(A_cumsum[..., -1:] - A_cumsum) 4. Compute states = sum_l B_blocks * decay_states * X_blocks -> `(b, c, h, d_head, d_state)` - Actually from the einsum: states[b,c,h,p,n] = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p] 5. Compute chunk_vec = A_cumsum[..., -1] -> `(b, h, c)` 6. Compute padded = F.pad(chunk_vec, (1, 0)) -> `(b, h, c+1)` 7. Compute chunk_cumsum = cumsum(padded, dim=-1) 8. Compute chunk_decays = exp(chunk_cumsum[..., -1:] - chunk_cumsum[..., 1:]) -> `(b, h, c)` - Note: chunk_cumsum[...,1:] means index 1 to c inclusive, shape c - chunk_cumsum[...,-1:] means last index, shape 1 9. Compute final_state = einsum("bhc,bchpn->bhpn", chunk_decays, states) -> `(b, h, d_head, d_state)` 10. Return final_state Wait, is there an initial state contribution? initial_states was zero. If it were non-zero, it would contribute `exp(M[c,0]) * initial_states = exp(chunk_cumsum[-1] - chunk_cumsum[0]) * initial_states = exp(chunk_cumsum[-1]) * initial_states` (since chunk_cumsum[0]=0, because padded starts with 0 and no previous elements). But since initial_states is zeros, this term is zero. But what if the user provides initial_states? In the reference: ```python if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1]) states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1) ... new_states = einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states) return new_states[:, -1] ``` If initial_states is non-zero, then `states_cat` = [initial_states, states_0, states_1, ...] And `new_states[:, -1] = sum_{c} decay_chunk[:,:,-1,c] * states_cat[:,c]` The c=0 term is `decay_chunk[:,:,-1,0] * initial_states`. `decay_chunk[:,:,-1,0] = exp(cumsum[-1] - cumsum[0]) = exp(cumsum[-1])`. So the contribution is `exp(cumsum[-1]) * initial_states`. So the full formula with non-zero initial states is: `new_states[:, -1] = exp(cumsum[-1]) * initial_states + sum_{j=0}^{c-1} exp(cumsum[-1] - cumsum[j+1]) * states[:,j]` = `exp(chunk_cumsum[:,:,-1:]) * initial_states + einsum("bhc,bchpn->bhpn", decay_factors, states)` where `decay_factors = exp(chunk_cumsum[:,:,-1:] - chunk_cumsum[:,:,1:])` shape `(b, h, c)`. And `exp(chunk_cumsum[:,:,-1:])` = `exp(sum(chunk_vec))`. So we can support initial_states efficiently too. This is a massive reduction in compute complexity. The reference computes: - A segsum for chunks of size (c+1)^2 -> O(c^2) per batch, head - A matrix multiply of (c+1, c+1) x effectively (c+1, p*n) -> O(c^2 * p * n) per batch, head - And Y_diag which is O(c * l^2 * h * p * n) or something large. Our optimized version computes: - O(c * l) for A_cumsum - O(c * l) for decay_states - O(c * l * h * p * n) for states (sum over l) - O(c) for chunk decays - O(c * h * p * n) for final einsum This is much faster. Now, the task is to write a CUDA kernel for this. But do we even need a custom CUDA kernel? The optimized PyTorch operations might already be faster than the reference. But we need a custom CUDA kernel via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Looking at the reference, it uses einsum, cumsum, exp, rearrange, cat, etc. Since our optimized ops are just a few elementwise ops and a contraction over l, we could write a fused CUDA kernel that does everything in one go. The original reference also computes Y_diag: `Y_diag = einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C, B, L, X)` This is: for each (b,c,l,h,p), sum over s and n: C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,l,s] * X[b,c,s,h,p]. Complexity: b * c * l * h * p * (l * n) = 2048 * 2 * 64 * 8 * 64 * (64 * 16) = 2048 * 2 * 64 * 8 * 64 * 1024 = 2 * 2048 * 8 * 64 * 64 * 1024 = 4096 * 8 * 64 * 65536 = 32768 * 4194304 = 137,438,953,472 ≈ 137 billion ops! This is huge! Computing Y_diag is extremely expensive. And it's thrown away. So skipping Y_diag alone will give a massive speedup. Therefore, even if we just implement the remaining ops in PyTorch, we'll be much faster than the reference. But we need to use a custom CUDA kernel. We can write a kernel that computes the entire forward pass in one fused operation. Actually, for the specific sizes: - b = 2048 - c = 2 - l = 64 - h = 8 - p = 64 (d_head) - n = 16 (d_state) The states computation is: states[b, c, h, p, n] = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(A_cumsum[b,h,c,-1] - A_cumsum[b,h,c,l]) * X[b,c,l,h,p] Since A_cumsum depends on A[b,h,c,l] (cumsum over l), we can precompute A_cumsum on GPU. fused kernel plan: 1. Reshape inputs 2. Compute A_cumsum per block via prefix sum (in shared memory or serially since l=64 is small) 3. Compute decay = exp(last_A - A_cumsum[l]) 4. For each (b, c, h, p, n), compute dot product over l: sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay[l] * X[b,c,l,h,p] Then compute chunk contributions: 5. chunk_vec[c] = A_cumsum[c, -1] 6. Compute chunk prefix sums, compute chunk_decays 7. final_state[b,h,p,n] = sum_c states[b,c,h,p,n] * chunk_decay[b,h,c] We can fuse steps 2-4 into one kernel, and 5-7 into another (or all into one). Given that l=64 is small, we can have each thread block handle one (b, c, h) and compute all (p, n) outputs. That would be p*n = 64*16 = 1024 outputs. With e.g. 256 threads per block, each thread computes 4 outputs. But wait, X is fp32, so memory bandwidth: reading X, B, A to compute states. X: b*c*l*h*p = 2048*2*64*8*64 = 134,217,728 elements = ~512 MB B: b*c*l*h*n = 2048*2*64*8*16 = 33,554,432 elements = ~128 MB A: b*h*c*l = 2048*8*2*64 = 2,097,152 elements = ~8 MB Memory bandwidth could be the bottleneck. But the output is b*c*h*p*n = 2048*2*8*64*16 = 33,554,432 elements. If we fuse, we might read X, B once and write states. Then read states and do the chunk reduction. Can we avoid writing states to global memory? We could compute the final output directly in one kernel if we process all c sequentially. For fixed (b, h, p, n): final = sum_c [chunk_decay_c * sum_l (B_{b,c,l,h,n} * decay_{b,c,l} * X_{b,c,l,h,p})] = sum_c sum_l [chunk_decay_c * B_{b,c,l,h,n} * decay_{b,c,l} * X_{b,c,l,h,p}] = sum_{c,l} [combined_decay_{b,c,l} * B_{b,c,l,h,n} * X_{b,c,l,h,p}] where combined_decay_{b,c,l} = chunk_decay_{b,h,c} * decay_{b,h,c,l} decay_{b,h,c,l} = exp(A_cumsum[b,h,c,-1] - A_cumsum[b,h,c,l]) chunk_decay_{b,h,c} = exp(chunk_cumsum[b,h,-1] - chunk_cumsum[b,h,c+1]) Note that chunk_cumsum is computed from chunk_vec = A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1]. chunk_cumsum[b,h,k] = sum_{i=0}^{k-1} A_cumsum[b,h,i,-1] for k>=1, and 0 for k=0. Wait, no: chunk_vec = A_cumsum[..., -1], so pad with (1,0) and cumsum: padded = [0, A_cumsum[...,0,-1], A_cumsum[...,1,-1], ...] chunk_cumsum[k] = sum_{i=0}^{k-1} A_cumsum[..., i, -1] chunk_cumsum[-1] = sum_{i=0}^{c-1} A_cumsum[..., i, -1] chunk_decay_c = exp(chunk_cumsum[-1] - chunk_cumsum[c+1]) = exp(sum_{i=c}^{c-1} A_cumsum[...,i,-1])? Wait. chunk_cumsum[-1] = total_sum chunk_cumsum[c+1] = sum_{i=0}^{c} A_cumsum[...,i,-1] ? No: c indexes chunks 0..c-1. There are c chunks. padded = [0, v0, v1, ..., v_{c-1}] where v_i = A_cumsum[...,i,-1] chunk_cumsum[k] = sum_{i=0}^{k-1} padded[i] = sum_{i=0}^{k-2} v_i for k>=1, and 0 for k=0. So for k from 0 to c: chunk_cumsum[0] = 0 chunk_cumsum[1] = v0 chunk_cumsum[2] = v0+v1 ... chunk_cumsum[c] = v0+...+v_{c-2} chunk_cumsum[c+1] = v0+...+v_{c-1} = total_sum Then chunk_decay for actual chunk index j (0-indexed) corresponds to: We had earlier: decay_factors = exp(chunk_cumsum[-1:] - chunk_cumsum[:,:,1:]) chunk_cumsum[:,:,1:] has indices 1, 2, ..., c. For chunk j (j from 0 to c-1), this corresponds to chunk_cumsum[j+1]. So chunk_decay_j = exp(total_sum - chunk_cumsum[j+1]) = exp(total_sum - sum_{i=0}^{j} v_i) = exp(sum_{i=j+1}^{c-1} v_i) So yes, chunk_decay_j = exp(sum of v's after chunk j). For c=2: chunk_decay_0 = exp(v1) chunk_decay_1 = exp(0) = 1 combined_decay for chunk 0, position l: = exp(v1) * exp(v0 - A_cumsum[0,l]) = exp(v0 + v1 - A_cumsum[0,l]) = exp(total_sum - A_cumsum[0,l]) combined_decay for chunk 1, position l: = 1 * exp(v1 - A_cumsum[1,l]) = exp(v1 - A_cumsum[1,l]) So for the last chunk, it's just exp(A_cumsum[1,-1] - A_cumsum[1,l]). For earlier chunks, it's exp(total_sum - A_cumsum[0,l]). Wait, A_cumsum[0,l] is the cumulative sum of A within chunk 0 up to position l. total_sum - A_cumsum[0,l] = [sum of all A in chunk 0 after position l] + [sum of all A in chunk 1]. So combined_decay represents the total decay from position (c,l) to the end of the sequence. Then: final_state[b,h,p,n] = sum_{c,l} B[b,c,l,h,n] * combined_decay[b,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p] For c=2: = sum_l B[0,l] * exp(total_sum - A_cumsum[0,l]) * X[0,l] + sum_l B[1,l] * exp(v1 - A_cumsum[1,l]) * X[1,l] This is nice! So we can compute in one pass over all (c,l) positions. The combined decay can be computed efficiently: - Compute A prefix sums within each chunk - Compute chunk totals v_c = A_cumsum[c,-1] - Compute suffix sums of chunk totals: suffix_sum[c] = sum_{i=c}^{c-1} v_i - Then combined_decay[c,l] = exp(suffix_sum[c] + v_c - A_cumsum[c,l]) Wait: For chunk c: suffix_sum[c] = sum_{i=c+1}^{c-1} v_i (0 if c is last chunk) v_c = chunk total A_cumsum[c,l] = prefix sum within chunk up to l So total sum after (c,l) = suffix_sum[c] + (v_c - A_cumsum[c,l]) combined_decay[c,l] = exp(suffix_sum[c] + v_c - A_cumsum[c,l]) For the last chunk c-1: suffix_sum = 0, so exp(v_{c-1} - A_cumsum[c-1,l]). This means we can compute everything in one kernel: 1. Load A block for a chunk, compute prefix sums in shared memory 2. Let's draft the kernel more carefully. ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void mamba_ssd_forward_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ A, // (B, H, C, L) const scalar_t* __restrict__ B, // (B, C, L, H, N) const scalar_t* __restrict__ X, // (B, C, L, H, P) scalar_t* __restrict__ out, // (B, H, P, N) int Bsz, int H, int C, int L, int N, int P ) { // One block per (b, h) int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; // Shared memory layout: // smem_A: C * L // smem_decay: C * L (or computed on the fly) // smem_B: C * L * N // smem_X: C * L * P // We also need v_suffix or can compute decay directly. extern __shared__ char smem[]; // Let's be careful with alignment // Actually, with C=2, L=64, N=16, P=64 } ``` But we need to make sure the kernel works for general C. Given C is small (seq_length / block_len), we can process any C. The shared memory will be: C*L + C*L + C*L*N + C*L*P floats. With C=2, L=64, N=16, P=64: 128 + 128 + 2048 + 8192 = 10,496 floats = 41,984 bytes. With C=4, L=64, N=16, P=64: 256 + 256 + 4096 + 16384 = 20,992 floats = 83,968 bytes > 48KB. So if C is larger, shared memory might not fit. We could tile over C. But in the benchmark C=2 only. To be safe, we could process one chunk at a time to reduce shared memory. Strategy: Process one chunk per iteration, keeping running accumulator in registers. Shared memory per iteration: L + L*N + L*P = 64 + 1024 + 4096 = 5184 floats = 20.7 KB. Much less! We can have more blocks per SM. Procedure per block (b, h): 1. Initialize accum[N_per_thread][P_per_thread] = 0 (in registers) 2. For c in 0..C-1: a. Load A[b, h, c, :] into smem_A[L] b. We can precompute all v_c first, then loop again, or store v_c and compute decay as exp(total_suffix + v_c - prefix[l]). Store v[c] = smem_A[c][L-1] after prefix sum. 4. Compute suffix sums: suffix[C-1] = 0; for c=C-2..0: suffix[c] = suffix[c+1] + v[c+1] So total_sum_after[c,l] = suffix[c] + v[c] - prefix[c][l] 5. Then for c=0..C-1: a. Load B[b,c,:,h,:] to smem_B b. Load X[b,c,:,h,:] to smem_X c. Compute decay_l = exp(suffix[c] + v[c] - smem_A[c][l]) for all l. We can store in smem_decay[L] or compute on the fly. On the fly: each thread computes decay for the l it's reading. Since we iterate l in the inner loop? No. Actually in the inner product over l, we can have each thread iterate l and read B[l][n], X[l][p], and use decay[l]. decay[l] = exp(suffix[c] + v[c] - smem_A[c][l]) We can compute this once and store in smem_decay[L]. That's another 256 bytes per block (L=64 floats). Negligible. d. __syncthreads() e. Each thread accumulates for its assigned (n,p): for l=0..L-1: accum += smem_B[l][n] * smem_X[l][p] * smem_decay[l] f. __syncthreads() 6. Write accum to out[b, h, p, n]. This works perfectly and uses little shared memory. Let's figure out thread count. Output per block: P * N = 64 * 16 = 1024 elements. Options: - 1024 threads, 1 element each - 512 threads, 2 elements each - 256 threads, 4 elements each - 128 threads, 8 elements each To maximize occupancy, fewer threads per block is better (more blocks concurrent per SM). With 21KB shared memory, an SM with 164KB shared can run up to 7 blocks. With 256 threads, each block uses 256 threads * something. Let's see GPU specs. Let me analyze the reference model carefully. The model is implementing a Structured State Space model (Mamba-like). Let me trace the operations: 1. Input X has shape (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head) 2. Parameters: A (batch, seq, n_heads), B (batch, seq, n_heads, d_state), C (batch, seq, n_heads, d_state) 3. A_cumsum is cumulative sum over block_len dimension Operations: 1. That's wasted computation in the reference. For our kernel, we only need to return `new_states[:, -1]`. So the computation we need is: 1. Compute A_cumsum from A_blocks 2. Compute decay_states = exp(A_cumsum[..., -1:] - A_cumsum) 3. states = einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B, decay_states, X) = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay_states[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p] 4. Actually step 6 is very small: 3x3 matrix times vector. For step 3: for each (b, c, h, p, n), sum over l of B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p]. This is essentially a batched elementwise product and sum. Let me think about how to fuse this efficiently. For each batch element b, chunk c, head h: - B has shape (block_len, d_state) - X has shape (block_len, d_head) - decay has shape (block_len,) - Output should be (d_state, d_head) - This is: sum_l decay[l] * B[l, n] * X[l, p] over l This is like: (decay * B)^T @ X, where decay is broadcast along d_state. Using batch matrix multiply would be efficient. So it's (16, 64) @ (64, 64) = (16, 64). Yes. But we can also think of this as a batch of batch_matmuls where we group by batch and head. For step 6 (inter-chunk): - decay_chunk has shape (2048, 8, 3, 3) - states has shape (2048, 3, 8, 16, 64) - We want new_states[b, z, h, p, n] = sum_c decay_chunk[b, h, z, c] * states[b, c, h, p, n] - This is a batch matmul: for each (b, h, p, n), decay_chunk[b, h, :, :] (3x3) times states[b, :, h, p, n] (3x1) -> (3x1) - Number of such operations: 2048 * 8 * 16 * 64 = 16,777,216, but each is only 3x3x3=27 multiplications. - Total FLOPs: ~450M, very small. - We can easily fuse this into the kernel. For a 1D SSD recurrence with 3 elements and decay_chunk being a causal lower-triangular matrix: new_states[:, 0] = decay_chunk[:, :, 0, 0] * s0 new_states[:, 1] = decay_chunk[:, :, 1, 0] * s0 + decay_chunk[:, :, 1, 1] * s1 new_states[:, 2] = decay_chunk[:, :, 2, 0] * s0 + decay_chunk[:, :, 2, 1] * s1 + decay_chunk[:, :, 2, 2] * s2 Since the diagonal of a segsum with cumsum on a padded sequence should be all 0s (because x_cumsum[:, i] - x_cumsum[:, i] = 0, and exp(0) = 1), actually decay_chunk diagonal elements = 1. Wait, segsum computes x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :]. The diagonal is all 0. So L_diagonal = exp(0) = 1. But segsum does masked_fill with -inf for upper triangle. So for the padding case with F.pad(A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1], (1,0)), the first element corresponds to the padding (0), and A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1] is the final cumsum for each chunk. Let's trace A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1] shape: (batch, heads, chunks=2) After pad with (1,0): (batch, heads, 3), where first element is 0. segsum computes cumsum differences: For element i and j in the padded array p (length 3): p = [0, a0, a1] where a0, a1 are the last cumsum values for chunk 0 and 1. segsum[i,j] = p[j] - p[i] for j <= i, else -inf. So: - decay_chunk[:,:,0,0] = exp(0) = 1 - decay_chunk[:,:,1,0] = exp(a0 - 0) = exp(a0) - decay_chunk[:,:,1,1] = exp(0) = 1 - decay_chunk[:,:,2,0] = exp(a1 - 0) = exp(a1) - decay_chunk[:,:,2,1] = exp(a1 - a0) - decay_chunk[:,:,2,2] = exp(0) = 1 new_states[:, 0] = 1 * s0 new_states[:, 1] = exp(a0) * s0 + 1 * s1 new_states[:, 2] = exp(a1) * s0 + exp(a1-a0) * s1 + 1 * s2 We return new_states[:, 2]. So the output for each (b, h) is: output = exp(a1[b,h]) * s0 + exp(a1[b,h]-a0[b,h]) * s1 + s2 Where s0 = 0 (initial_states). So: output = exp(a1[b,h]-a0[b,h]) * s1 + s2 And: - a0 = A_cumsum[b, h, 0, -1] = sum of A[b, 0, :, h] over block_len - a1 = A_cumsum[b, h, 1, -1] = sum of A[b, 1, :, h] over block_len - exp(a1 - a0) = exp(sum_l A[b, 1, l, h] - sum_l A[b, 0, l, h]) = product over l of exp(A[b,1,l,h]) / product over l of exp(A[b,0,l,h]) Wait, A_cumsum is the cumsum over the block dimension. So A_cumsum[b,h,c,-1] is the sum of A_blocks[b,c,:,h] (since A was rearranged). So we don't even need to compute the full segsum for the chunk recurrence! We can compute this much more directly. For the return value: For each batch b, head h: decay_factor = exp(A_cumsum[b,h,1,-1] - A_cumsum[b,h,0,-1]) output[b,h,:,:] = decay_factor * states[b,0,h,:,:] + states[b,1,h,:,:] Where states[b,c,h,n,p] = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(A_cumsum[b,h,c,-1] - A_cumsum[b,h,c,l]) * X[b,c,l,h,p] And A_cumsum[b,h,c,l] = sum_{k=0}^{l} A[b,c,k,h] So for the main computation: For each b, c, h: decay_states[b,h,c,l] = exp(A_cumsum[b,h,c,-1] - A_cumsum[b,h,c,l]) states[b,c,h] = (B[b,c,:,h,:] * decay_states[b,h,c,:][:,None]).T @ X[b,c,:,h,:] This gives states of shape (batch, chunks, heads, d_state, d_head). Then for the return: out = states[:,1] + exp(A_cumsum[:,:,1,-1] - A_cumsum[:,:,0,-1])[:,:,None,None] * states[:,0] Wait, shape check: states: (2048, 2, 8, 16, 64) output: (2048, 8, 16, 64) decay_factor: (2048, 8) out[b,h,n,p] = states[b,1,h,n,p] + exp(a1[b,h]-a0[b,h]) * states[b,0,h,n,p] Yes this matches. So the entire forward computation is: 1. A_blocks: A is already in the right shape after rearrange, but we need to compute cumsum. 2. For each chunk, compute states via the elementwise product and matmul. 3. Combine states with the decay factor. We can fuse all of this into a single kernel! Let me think about the kernel design: For each (b, h) there are 2 chunks. For each chunk c: - Compute A_cumsum along l=64 - Compute decay_states[l] = exp(A_cumsum[63] - A_cumsum[l]) - Compute states as a matmul: states[n, p] = sum_l B[l, n] * decay_states[l] * X[l, p] For the decay factor between chunks: - a0 = A_cumsum for chunk 0, last element - a1 = A_cumsum for chunk 1, last element - factor = exp(a1 - a0) - output = states_chunk1 + factor * states_chunk0 To maximize throughput, I can have each thread block handle one (b, h) pair and process both chunks. Since there are 2048 * 8 = 16384 such pairs, we have plenty of parallelism. For each (b, h), the work per chunk is: - dot products of B (64x16) with X (64x64), weighted by decay: effectively a (16x64) @ (64x64) = 16x64 matmul. - 2 chunks per (b, h) - Total per (b, h): 2 * 16 * 64 * 64 = 131K FLOPs - For 16384 (b, h): total 2.1 GFLOPs, very small. Actually, since the total work is small, memory bandwidth is likely the bottleneck. We need to minimize memory traffic. The inputs per (b, h, c): - A: 64 floats - B: 64 * 16 = 1024 floats - X: 64 * 64 = 4096 floats Total read per (b, h): 2 * (64 + 1024 + 4096) = 10368 floats = 41.5 KB For 16384 (b, h): ~680 MB of reads Output: 16 * 64 = 1024 floats per (b, h) = 64 MB total. So this is memory bandwidth bound. We need to fuse everything and minimize writes. Kernel design: - Grid: (batch_size * n_heads) - Each block handles one (b, h) and both chunks. - We can load A for both chunks, compute cumsum and decay, then compute the small matmul. For the matmul (16, 64) @ (64, 64): - Each output is sum over l=64. - We can have a thread block of say 128 threads, and compute the output cooperatively. - Since d_state=16 and d_head=64, total output per chunk is 1024 elements. - With 128 threads, each thread computes 8 output elements. - Or we can use shared memory for A and load B/X in tiles. Actually, a simpler approach: for each (b, h, c), and for each n in [0, 16) and p in [0, 64): states[n, p] = sum_l B[l, n] * decay[l] * X[l, p] We can compute this as: For each l from 0 to 63: decay_l = exp(last_A - A_cumsum[l]) For each n in [0, 16), p in [0, 64): states[n, p] += B[l, n] * decay_l * X[l, p] Since 16*64 = 1024 outputs per chunk, and l=64, we do 1024*64 = 65536 multiply-adds per chunk. For 2 chunks: 131K operations per (b, h). If we have 128 threads per block, each thread can handle 8 outputs (for one chunk or across chunks). Another approach: use warp-level parallelism. Each warp computes a subset of (n, p) pairs. Let me design the kernel: ```cuda template <int BLOCK_SIZE> __global__ void mamba_ssd_kernel( const float* X, // (B, C, L, H, P) const float* A, // (B, C, L, H) const float* B, // (B, C, L, H, N) float* out, // (B, H, N, P) int B_, int C, int L, int H, int N, int P ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; // Each block processes both chunks for this (b, h) // We'll compute states for chunk 0 and chunk 1 in shared memory or registers // Load A for both chunks, compute cumsum and decay // Then compute states = B^T @ (decay * X) // For thread parallelism: we'll have threads work on different (n, p) pairs // Total outputs: 2 * N * P = 2 * 16 * 64 = 2048 float accumulators // If block has 256 threads, each handles 8 accumulators. } ``` Wait, but with 16384 blocks and only about 132 SMs on H100, each SM processes ~124 blocks. That's fine since each block has small work. Actually, let me count: H100 has 132 SMs, each can run up to 32 blocks. Total concurrent blocks ~4224. We have 16384 blocks, so each SM runs about 4 waves. That's fine. To maximize memory efficiency, let's use a moderate block size and have each thread compute a few output elements. Actually, for such small matmuls, using Tensor Cores might be overkill. A simpler vectorized approach should work well. Key insight: since we have 16384 independent units of work (each (b, h) pair), and each has 131K FLOPs, we can parallelize at the block level. Let me design the kernel: For each block representing (b, h): 1. Allocate shared memory for A of both chunks: 2 * 64 = 128 floats. Very small. 2. Allocate shared memory for decay of both chunks: 128 floats. 3. For each chunk c: - Load A[b, c, 0:L, h] into shared memory. - Compute prefix sum (cumsum) in shared memory. Since L=64, we can do this efficiently with parallel scan or just sequentially since it's so small. - Compute decay[l] = exp(A[L-1] - A[l]) for each l. 4. Now compute states for both chunks. - For chunk c, states[n, p] = sum_l B[b, c, l, h, n] * decay[c, l] * X[b, c, l, h, p] - Total outputs: 2 * N * P = 2048 floats. 5. Compute output = states_chunk1 + exp(A_chunk1_last - A_chunk0_last) * states_chunk0 6. Write output to out[b, h, :, :]. For memory access patterns: - X is accessed as X[b, c, l, h, p] with strides. Need to know the layout. - A is (B, C, L, H), so A[b, c, l, h] = A[((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h] - B is (B, C, L, H, N), so B[b, c, l, h, n] = B[(((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * N + n] - X is (B, C, L, H, P), similar. - out is (B, H, N, P) Wait, I need to check the original rearrange operations to know the exact layouts: Original: ```python X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [ rearrange(x, "b (c l) ... -> b c l ...", l=self.block_len) for x in (X, self.A, self.B, self.C) ] A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l") ``` So: - X_blocks: (B, C, L, H, P) - A_blocks: (B, H, C, L) - B_blocks: (B, C, L, H, N) - C_blocks: (B, C, L, H, N) but not needed for output In the reference, `states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks)`. So B is (B, C, L, H, N), decay_states is (B, H, C, L), X is (B, C, L, H, P). For the einsum, the result is (B, C, H, P, N). But we established we don't need Y_diag, only the states. And `new_states[:, -1]` has shape (B, H, P, N). Wait, I need to double check. The return is `new_states[:, -1]` where new_states has shape (B, Z, H, P, N) - wait let me check. ```python new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states) ``` So new_states is (B, Z, H, P, N) where Z = C+1 = 3. Then `new_states[:, -1]` is (B, H, P, N). Hmm, I thought it was (B, H, N, P). Let me check: states is (B, C, H, P, N)? `states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", ...)` bclhn + bhcl + bclhp -> bchpn So: b, c, h, p, n. Yes, states is (B, C, H, P, N). Then output is (B, H, P, N). So out layout should be (B, H, P, N) = (2048, 8, 64, 16). And the computation is: states[b, c, h, p, n] = sum_l B[b, c, l, h, n] * decay[b, h, c, l] * X[b, c, l, h, p] Which is: for each (b, c, h), compute (P, N) matrix where: M[p, n] = sum_l (decay[l] * X[l, p]) * B[l, n] = X'[l, p] * B[l, n] where X' = decay[:, None] * X This is X'.T @ B... wait no. X' has shape (L, P), B has shape (L, N). sum over l of X'[l, p] * B[l, n] = (X'.T @ B)[p, n] -> shape (P, N). Yes, so M = X'^T @ B, where X' = decay[:, None] * X. X has shape (L, P) = (64, 64), B has shape (L, N) = (64, 16). So it's a (64, 64) tensor transposed to (64, 64) and multiplied by (64, 16)? Wait, (X'.T) is (P, L) = (64, 64), B is (L, N) = (64, 16). Result is (P, N) = (64, 16). But earlier I said B' was (64, 16), and B'.T @ X gives (16, 64). This is different! Let me re-check. einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B, decay, X) For fixed b, c, h: sum over l, n of? Wait, the output index is p, n, and input has n on B. einsum: B_bclhn * decay_bhcl * X_bclhp -> sum over l: B[l, n] * decay[l] * X[l, p] = sum_l B[l, n] * decay[l] * X[l, p] For fixed n, p: = sum_l B[l, n] * (decay[l] * X[l, p]) = (B^T @ (decay * X))[n, p] ? B is (L, N), so B^T is (N, L). decay*X is (L, P). B^T @ (decay*X) is (N, P). But the output should be (P, N) or (N, P)? Wait, the einsum output is bchpn, which for fixed b, c, h gives (p, n). So output[p, n] = sum_l B[l, n] * decay[l] * X[l, p] This can be written as: output = (decay[:, None] * X).T @ B = X'^T @ B where X' = decay * X elementwise along L. X is (L, P), X'^T is (P, L), B is (L, N). Result: (P, N). Alternatively: output[p, n] = X'[:, p] · B[:, n] Either way, it's a matmul of (P, L) @ (L, N) = (P, N). Or equivalently, for the transposed output: (output.T)[n, p] = B^T @ X' = (N, L) @ (L, P) = (N, P). So output.T = B^T @ (decay[:, None] * X). Since N=16, P=64, L=64: B^T is (16, 64), X' is (64, 64), result is (16, 64). This is (16, 64) @ (64, 64) = (16, 64). This is a very small matmul: M=16, K=64, N=64. And we have batch_size * chunks * heads = 2048 * 2 * 8 = 32768 such matmuls. This is actually a great case for cublas batched gemm or custom kernel. Since the matrices are very small, a custom kernel will be faster because cublas has overhead for small batches. For our kernel, each block (b, h) handles 2 such matmuls. Let me design the kernel with Tensor Cores if possible, or just a simple efficient custom kernel. Given H100 has amazing Tensor Cores, we should try to use them. But for such small M=16, K=64, N=64, a simple warp-mma approach might be worth it. Actually, given the simplicity, let me write a straightforward kernel: - 128 threads per block - Each block handles one (b, h) - Load A for both chunks, compute cumsum and decay in shared memory - Load B and X, compute the matmul using shared memory tiling For the (16, 64) @ (64, 64) matmul: - We can tile with 16x16 blocks. - B^T is (16, 64), X' is (64, 64). - We want output.T which is (16, 64). Actually, a better approach: use shared memory for X'. Since X' is (64, 64), that's 4096 floats = 16KB. We have 228KB shared memory, so we can easily hold both chunks' X' in shared memory. Similarly, B is (64, 16) = 4KB per chunk. We can also hold both in shared memory. And A is (64,) per chunk = 256 bytes. So for each block: - shm_A: 128 floats (512 bytes) - shm_decay: 128 floats (512 bytes) - shm_B: 2 * 64 * 16 = 2048 floats (8KB) - shm_X: 2 * 64 * 64 = 8192 floats (32KB) Total shared memory: ~41KB. Well within limits. But loading X into shared memory for each block might not be optimal if we can stream through L and compute incrementally. Let me think about a simpler kernel design: For each block (b, h): // Step 1: Load A for both chunks, compute cumsum and decay for c = 0 to 1: Load A[b, h, c, 0:64] to shm_A cumsum in registers or shared memory decay[c, l] = exp(cumsum[63] - cumsum[l]) // Step 2: Initialize accumulators states[2][16][64] = {0} // per chunk, per n, per p // Step 3: For each chunk, compute states for c = 0 to 1: for l = 0 to 63: decay_l = decay[c, l] for n = 0 to 15: B_l_n = B[b, c, l, h, n] for p = 0 to 63: states[c][n][p] += B_l_n * decay_l * X[b, c, l, h, p] // Step 4: Combine and write output factor = exp(A_cumsum[1][63] - A_cumsum[0][63]) for n = 0 to 15, p = 0 to 63: out[b, h, p, n] = states[1][n][p] + factor * states[0][n][p] To parallelize this across threads, we can have each thread handle a subset of (n, p) pairs. Wait, we need A_cumsum for: 1. decay[l] = exp(A_cumsum[63] - A_cumsum[l]) 2. factor = exp(A_cumsum_chunk1[63] - A_cumsum_chunk0[63]) We can compute A_cumsum sequentially in one thread, then broadcast to others. Since L=64 is tiny, this is fine. Kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void ssd_kernel( const float* __restrict__ X, const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ B, float* __restrict__ out, int B_, int C, int L, int H, int N, int P ) { __shared__ float shm_A[2 * 64]; __shared__ float shm_decay[2 * 64]; __shared__ float factor; int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; // Thread mapping int tx = threadIdx.x; int n = tx / 8; int p0 = (tx % 8) * 8; // Load A for both chunks and compute cumsum // Only first few threads do this if (tx < 2 * L) { int c = tx / L; int l = tx % L; int idx = ((b * H + h) * C + c) * L + l; shm_A[tx] = A[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute cumsum for chunk 0 if (tx == 0) { float cumsum = 0; for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { cumsum += shm_A[l]; shm_A[l] = cumsum; } float last0 = shm_A[L-1]; float cumsum1 = 0; for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { cumsum1 += shm_A[L + l]; shm_A[L + l] = cumsum1; } float last1 = shm_A[2*L - 1]; factor = expf(last1 - last0); for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { shm_decay[l] = expf(last0 - shm_A[l]); shm_decay[L + l] = expf(last1 - shm_A[L + l]); } } __syncthreads(); float factor_val = factor; // Now compute states for both chunks float acc0[8] = {0}; float acc1[8] = {0}; // We can process both chunks in one loop, alternating // Or just loop over l and do both chunks for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { // Chunk 0 int b_idx = (((b * C + 0) * L + l) * H + h) * N + n; float B_val0 = B[b_idx]; float decay0 = shm_decay[l]; B_val0 *= decay0; int x_base0 = (((b * C + 0) * L + l) * H + h) * P + p0; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 8; k++) { acc0[k] += B_val0 * X[x_base0 + k]; So the computation is exactly: for z in [0, 1, 2] (the output index): new_states[b, z, h, p, n] = sum_c decay_chunk[b, h, z, c] * states[b, c, h, p, n] For z=2 (the last one): = decay_chunk[b,h,2,0] * states[b,0,h,p,n] + decay_chunk[b,h,2,1] * states[b,1,h,p,n] + decay_chunk[b,h,2,2] * states[b,2,h,p,n] = exp(a0+a1) * initial + exp(a1) * states0 + 1 * states1. Perfect. Now, one more numerical note: A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1] is the last element. cumsum over L gives the prefix sum. The last element is the sum of all elements in that chunk. So a0 = sum_l A[b,h,0,l], a1 = sum_l A[b,h,1,l]. In the kernel, I compute A_cumsum for chunk 0 and store it in shm_A[0..L-1]. The last element is at shm_A[L-1]. Similarly for chunk 1 at shm_A[2*L-1]. Since we only need the last elements (a0 and a1) for the inter-chunk factor, and the full cumsum for decay_states, we need to compute the full cumsum anyway. For efficiency, I can compute the cumsum and also compute the decay on the fly. Since L=64, thread 0 does all of this quickly. Actually, I don't even need to store the full A_cumsum. I can store just the running sum and compute decay as I go. But I need all decays for all l simultaneously when the matmul happens. So storing decay in shared memory is the right approach. Let me use this approach to be safe. Actually, wait. Re-reading the reference, `initial_states` has shape related to `states[:, :1]`. If initial_states is passed, it would need to be rearranged/reshaped too. But there's another issue: the kernel currently doesn't handle non-contiguous initial_states. I'll call `.contiguous()` on it. Let me finalize the code. Wait, I just noticed a potential performance issue. In the kernel, `X + x_base0` where x_base0 depends on b, c, l, h, p0. When consecutive blocks (along blockIdx.x) execute, they access different b and h values. Since blocks are independent and can execute in any order, the memory access pattern is fine but cache locality between blocks is limited. However, with large batch size, consecutive b values are adjacent in memory. Block bh and bh+1 have the same h but consecutive b, or the next h for the same b. Since b is the slowest dimension, consecutive bh values (with h wrapping) have b changing only every H blocks. So blocks 0..7 have b=0, h=0..7. Blocks 8..15 have b=1, h=0..7. Etc. For a given l and p0, X for block 0 at (b=0, h=0) and block 1 at (b=0, h=1) are separated by P elements in the h dimension. For P=64, this is 256 bytes. Consecutive blocks accessing memory 256 bytes apart. This should still be somewhat cache-coherent. For blocks with the same b and consecutive h, the X values for fixed l, p0 are at offsets h*P apart = 256 bytes. With H=8, a full set of h values spans 2KB. This is fine. The H100 cache line is likely 128 bytes, so consecutive h values might be in different cache lines. But with 2048 total threads per SM and good local memory scheduling, this should still perform well. Alright, let me write the code and test it. Actually, one more optimization: instead of processing both chunks in the same loop, I could process chunk 0 first, then chunk 1, to reduce register pressure. But since L=64 and the loop is small, keeping both might allow instruction-level parallelism. Also, I should consider prefetching. The compiler might do this automatically. I'll stick with the current design and test it. Let me write the code with the dummy tensor approach for initial_states.

Maybe the easiest correct and fast approach for general C is: __global__ void kernel(float* out, const float* X, const float* A, const float* B, const float* init, int B_, int C, int L, int H, int P, int N, bool has_init) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int pn = threadIdx.x; // 0..P*N-1 if (pn >= P * N) return; int p = pn / N; int n = pn % N; float acc = 0.0f; float running_decay = 1.0f; // First compute total A prefix to weight init if needed. // Actually simpler: compute all chunk states first, then combine. // But we don't have per-thread local storage for all s_c. // So let's do reverse accumulation: // for c = C-1 downto 0: // compute s_c // acc += s_c * running_decay // running_decay *= exp(A_cumsum_end_c) // after loop: if has_init: acc += init * running_decay for (int c = C - 1; c >= 0; --c) { // compute A_cumsum for chunk c float local_A[64]; // if L <= 64 // read A float a_cumsum = 0.0f; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float a = A[(((b * H + h) * C + c) * L + l)]; a_cumsum += a; local_A[l] = a_cumsum; } float a_end = a_cumsum; // compute s_c float s_c = 0.0f; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float decay = expf(a_end - local_A[l]); float b_val = B[((((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * N + n)]; float x_val = X[((((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * P + p)]; s_c += b_val * x_val * decay; } acc += s_c * running_decay; running_decay *= expf(a_end); } if (has_init) { acc += init[(((b * H + h) * P + p) * N + n)] * running_decay; } out[(((b * H + h) * P + p) * N + n)] = acc; } This is very simple and general. I need to understand the reference model more carefully. Let me trace through what it does: 1. Rearranges X, A, B, C into blocks 2. Computes A_cumsum 3. Returns `new_states[:, -1]` Wait, let me re-read the forward: ```python def forward(self, X, initial_states=None): X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [...] A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l") A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1) # 1. Compute diagonal block outputs... (but Y_diag is not used!) ... # 2. Compute intra-chunk states decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum)) states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks) # 3. Compute inter-chunk recurrence if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1]) states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1) decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0)))) new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states) return new_states[:, -1] ``` The key computation for `states` (intra-chunk): - `decay_states`: for each chunk c, each position l in the chunk: `exp(A_cumsum[c,-1] - A_cumsum[c,l])` - This equals `exp(sum_{i=l}^{L-1} A[c,i])` - `states = einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B, decay_states, X)` - This is `sum_{l=0}^{L-1} B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay_states[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p]` Then for inter-chunk recurrence: - `A_chunk = A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1]` is the cumulative sum within each chunk - `pad(A_chunk, (1,0))` adds a zero at the beginning: [0, chunk0_sum, chunk1_sum, ...] - `segsum` on this produces a lower-triangular matrix where entry (i,j) = `sum_{k=j}^{i-1} A_chunk[k]` - Actually `segsum` computes: `x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j]` for j <= i - So entry (i,j) = `sum_{k=j}^{i-1} A_chunk[k]` - `decay_chunk[b,h,z,c]` = `exp(sum_{k=c}^{z-1} A_chunk[b,h,k])` for z >= c - `new_states = einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states)` - `new_states[b,z,h,p,n] = sum_{c=0}^{z} decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] * states[b,c,h,p,n]` - Returns `new_states[:, -1]` So the full recurrence is: `new_states[b,z,h,p,n] = sum_{c=0}^{z} exp(sum_{k=c}^{z-1} A_chunk[b,h,k]) * states[b,c,h,p,n]` For z = C (last chunk): `new_states[:, -1, h, p, n] = sum_{c=0}^{C-1} exp(sum_{k=c}^{C-1} A_chunk[b,h,k]) * states[b,c,h,p,n]` And `states[b,c,h,p,n] = sum_{l=0}^{L-1} B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(sum_{i=l}^{L-1} A[b,c,i,h]) * X[b,c,l,h,p]` The final output is exactly: `out[b,h,p,n] = sum_{c=0}^{C-1} exp(sum_{k=c}^{C-1} A_chunk[b,h,k]) * sum_{l=0}^{L-1} B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(sum_{i=l}^{L-1} A[b,c,i,h]) * X[b,c,l,h,p]` Notice that: `exp(sum_{k=c}^{C-1} A_chunk[b,h,k]) = exp(sum_{k=c}^{C-1} sum_{l=0}^{L-1} A[b,h,k,l])` So the total decay for a position (c,l) is: `exp(sum_{i=l}^{L-1} A[b,c,i,h] + sum_{k=c+1}^{C-1} sum_{j=0}^{L-1} A[b,h,k,j])` This is exactly the total "future" sum of A from position (c,l) to the end of the sequence. So the output is: `out[b,h,p,n] = sum_{c=0}^{C-1} sum_{l=0}^{L-1} B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(sum_{(c',l') >= (c,l)} A[b,h,c',l']) * X[b,c,l,h,p]` where the sum over (c',l') is over all positions from (c,l) to the end. This is a reverse cumulative product structure. My kernel was computing the reverse, but let me check the indices carefully. Wait, I had `decay_states = exp(A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1:] - A_cumsum)` but I used it differently. Let me re-examine. In my kernel, I was iterating backwards from the last chunk. For each chunk c, I was computing: - `a_end = sum_{l=0}^{L-1} A[c,l]` ... this is A_chunk[c] - Then I computed `state` by iterating backwards within the chunk, accumulating decay. Let me reconsider. For the geometric product, going backwards is actually the right approach: - Start from the end, each position gets a decay equal to `exp(sum of future A)`. Let me write the kernel with one thread per (b,h,p,n): ```c __global__ void mamba_ssd_simple( const float* __restrict__ X, // (B, C, L, H, P) const float* __restrict__ A, // (B, H, C, L) const float* __restrict__ B, // (B, C, L, H, N) const float* __restrict__ initial_states, // (B, H, P, N) or null float* __restrict__ out, // (B, H, P, N) int B_, int C, int L, int H, int P, int N, bool has_init ) { // Grid: enough blocks for all (b,h,p,n) // Each thread: one (b,h,p,n) int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B_ * H * P * N; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode idx to (b, h, p, n) int n = idx % N; int tmp = idx / N; int p = tmp % P; tmp = tmp / P; int h = tmp % H; int b = tmp / H; // Compute total_A for this (b, h) float total_A = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { int a_base = ((b * H + h) * C + c) * L; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { total_A += A[a_base + l]; } } // Accumulate output float sum = 0.0f; float prefix = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { int a_base = ((b * H + h) * C + c) * L; for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) { float decay = expf(total_A - prefix); int x_idx = ((((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * P + p); float x_val = X[x_idx]; int b_idx = ((((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * N + n); float b_val = B[b_idx]; sum += b_val * decay * x_val; prefix += A[a_base + l]; } } if (has_init) { int init_idx = ((b * H + h) * P + p) * N + n; sum += initial_states[init_idx] * expf(total_A); } int out_idx = ((b * H + h) * P + p) * N + n; out[out_idx] = sum; } ``` This is very straightforward. Then `new_states[:, -1]` would be the final state after processing all chunks (and optionally initial state). Now my kernel computes: `sum += b_val * decay * x_val` where `decay = exp(total_A - prefix)`. This gives `sum(B[l] * decay * X[l])`. With initial state: `sum += init * exp(total_A)`. But `total_A` is the sum of all A values, and `prefix` is the prefix sum. So `decay = exp(sum(A[l+1:]))`. Let's trace it. `einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", D, S)` means for each output: `sum_c D[b,h,z,c] * S[b,c,h,p,n]`. So for `z` from 0 to C, and `c` from 0 to C. Since D has the triangular `segsum` matrix, this is a matrix-vector product with a lower triangular matrix (or rather, causal such that z >= c). The output is `(B, C+1, H, P, N)`. `new_states[:, z]` = sum over c of D[b,h,z,c] * S[:, c]. Since D is lower triangular (z >= c), D[b,h,z,c] = exp(sum of A_cumsum at end for chunks c to z-1) === exp(prefix_sum_A_to_z-1 - prefix_sum_A_to_c-1) ? For chunk indices c and z (where z >= c), `D[b,h,z,c] = exp(sum_{cc=c}^{z-1} A_chunk[cc])` where `A_chunk[cc]` is the sum of A in chunk cc. So `new_states[:, z,:,:,:]` = sum_{c=0}^{z-1} [exp(sum_{cc=c}^{z-1} A_chunk[cc]) * S[:, c]] where S[:, c] is the local state for chunk c (already computed with intra-decay). For `segsum(x)`: ```python T = len(x) x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1) x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :] mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T,T), diagonal=0) x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -inf) ``` For z (row), c (col): `segsum[z,c] = x_cumsum[z] - x_cumsum[c]` if z >= c else -inf. Since `diagonal=0` in `tril`, it's finite for z >= c. For z = c: `x_cumsum[z] - x_cumsum[c] = 0`. So `exp(0) = 1`. But wait: with `pad(..., (1,0))`, we pad with one element at the beginning. Actually, looking at the second kernel more carefully: ```cpp for (int c = C - 1; c >= 0; --c) { // ... float state[16]; #pragma unroll for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) state[n] = 0.0f; float local_decay = 1.0f; for (int l = L - 1; l >= 0; --l) { // ... for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { float b_val = B[b_idx + n]; state[n] += b_val * x_val * local_decay; } // ... } // ... } ``` The access pattern for B is `B[b_idx + n]`. b_idx depends on l. For each l, B is loaded N times. This is cache friendly if N is small. For X, each thread for a given p loads X for each l. Since X is of shape `(..., H, P)`, and different threads in the same block have different p, X is accessed with stride 1 across p... no, each thread loads its own p, so there is no coalescing for X across threads in the same warp since they all load the same address! Wait, each warp has 32 threads, and p can range from 0 to 63. But since each block only has 64 threads (P=64), and each thread handles a unique p, for a fixed (b,h,c,l), threads with different p load adjacent memory locations in X. X address for thread p: `((((b * C + c) * L + l) * H + h) * P + p)`. So consecutive p map to consecutive floats. 32 consecutive threads (e.g., p=0..31) will load 32 consecutive floats. This is fully coalesced! Great. For B, all threads in a warp load the same address for a given n? No, each thread loads `B[b_idx + n]` for n from 0 to N-1. Since b_idx doesn't depend on p, all threads load the same B values for a given n. This is broadcast access, which is also efficient. For A, `a_l = A[a_base + l]`, which is the same for all threads in the block. Also broadcast, efficient. So the memory access pattern of the second kernel is actually quite good! And the register count: P=64 threads per block. Each thread has: - acc[16] floats = 64 registers - state[16] floats = 64 registers - Plus temporales. Total maybe ~150 registers. Max 255, so it's fine. Actually, since acc and state are arrays of size N=16, each thread needs 32 floats just for those. On H100, each thread has 255 registers max (32-bit). 32 registers for acc+state is fine. But wait, with 64 threads per block and B_*H = 2048*8 = 16384 blocks, total parallelism is 16384 blocks. On H100 with ~132 SMs, this is good. So the second kernel should be correct. Let me test it. I also realize: in my second kernel I need to make sure the initial state shape handling is correct. The test with `get_inputs()` only passes `X`, no initial states. So `has_init=False`. Let me write the second kernel code properly.

c) * L;\n float a_end = 0.0f;\n for (int l = 0; l < L; ++l) {\n a_end += A[a_base + l];\n }\n float exp_a_end = expf(a_end);\n\n float state[16];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n)
